RME 4221: Introduction to Machine Learning — In-course Examination

Full Marks: 20 · Time: 1 Hour

Question 1 — Feed-forward Network: Forward Pass, Loss, Backpropagation & Update

10 marks
Network: 3 inputs → 2 hidden units (ReLU) → 2 outputs (Sigmoid). Inputs \(x=[0.6,\,0.9,\,0.4]^T\). \[W^{(1)}=\begin{bmatrix}0.4&-0.3\\0.2&0.5\\-0.6&0.1\end{bmatrix},\; b^{(1)}=\begin{bmatrix}0.1\\-0.2\end{bmatrix},\quad W^{(2)}=\begin{bmatrix}0.5&-0.4\\0.7&0.3\end{bmatrix},\; b^{(2)}=\begin{bmatrix}0.05\\-0.1\end{bmatrix}\]
(a) Hidden layer activations

Pre-activation: \(z^{(1)} = W^{(1)T}x + b^{(1)}\). Each hidden unit takes a weighted sum of all three inputs (column \(j\) of \(W^{(1)}\) holds the weights into hidden unit \(j\)):

\[z^{(1)}_1 = (0.4)(0.6) + (0.2)(0.9) + (-0.6)(0.4) + 0.1 = 0.24 + 0.18 - 0.24 + 0.1 = 0.28\] \[z^{(1)}_2 = (-0.3)(0.6) + (0.5)(0.9) + (0.1)(0.4) - 0.2 = -0.18 + 0.45 + 0.04 - 0.2 = 0.11\]

ReLU: \(h_j=\max(0, z^{(1)}_j)\). Both pre-activations are positive, so they pass unchanged.

Answer: \(z^{(1)}=[0.28,\;0.11]^T\), hence \(h=[0.28,\;0.11]^T\).
(b) Output layer activations

Pre-activation: \(z^{(2)} = W^{(2)}h + b^{(2)}\):

\[z^{(2)}_1 = (0.5)(0.28) + (-0.4)(0.11) + 0.05 = 0.14 - 0.044 + 0.05 = 0.146\] \[z^{(2)}_2 = (0.7)(0.28) + (0.3)(0.11) - 0.1 = 0.196 + 0.033 - 0.1 = 0.129\]

Sigmoid: \(\hat y_i = \sigma(z^{(2)}_i)=\dfrac{1}{1+e^{-z^{(2)}_i}}\):

\[\hat y_1 = \frac{1}{1+e^{-0.146}} = 0.5364,\qquad \hat y_2 = \frac{1}{1+e^{-0.129}} = 0.5322\]
Answer: \(\hat y = [0.5364,\;0.5322]^T\).
(c) Categorical cross-entropy loss, target \(y=[1,0]^T\)

\(\displaystyle L = -\sum_i y_i \ln \hat y_i = -\big(1\cdot\ln 0.5364 + 0\cdot \ln 0.5322\big) = -\ln(0.5364)\)

Answer: \(L = 0.6228\).
(d) Gradients w.r.t. \(W^{(2)}\) and \(b^{(2)}\)

Define \(\delta^{(2)} = \partial L/\partial z^{(2)}\). Since \(L=-\ln\hat y_1\) and \(\hat y_i=\sigma(z^{(2)}_i)\):

\[\frac{\partial L}{\partial \hat y_1} = -\frac{1}{\hat y_1} = -\frac{1}{0.5364} = -1.8642,\qquad \frac{\partial L}{\partial \hat y_2} = 0\] \[\sigma'(z^{(2)}_1)=\hat y_1(1-\hat y_1)=0.5364(0.4636)=0.2487,\qquad \sigma'(z^{(2)}_2)=0.5322(0.4678)=0.2490\] \[\delta^{(2)}_1 = (-1.8642)(0.2487) = -0.4636,\qquad \delta^{(2)}_2 = (0)(0.2490)=0\]

Then \(\dfrac{\partial L}{\partial W^{(2)}} = \delta^{(2)} h^{T}\) and \(\dfrac{\partial L}{\partial b^{(2)}} = \delta^{(2)}\):

\[\frac{\partial L}{\partial W^{(2)}} = \begin{bmatrix}-0.4636\\0\end{bmatrix}\begin{bmatrix}0.28 & 0.11\end{bmatrix} = \begin{bmatrix}-0.1298 & -0.0510\\ 0 & 0\end{bmatrix}\]
Answer: \(\nabla_{W^{(2)}}L=\begin{bmatrix}-0.1298 & -0.0510\\0 & 0\end{bmatrix}\), \(\nabla_{b^{(2)}}L=[-0.4636,\;0]^T\).
(e) Gradients w.r.t. \(W^{(1)}\) and \(b^{(1)}\)

Backpropagate through \(W^{(2)}\): \(\dfrac{\partial L}{\partial h} = W^{(2)T}\delta^{(2)}\):

\[\frac{\partial L}{\partial h_1} = (0.5)(-0.4636)+(0.7)(0) = -0.2318,\qquad \frac{\partial L}{\partial h_2} = (-0.4)(-0.4636)+(0.3)(0)=0.1854\]

ReLU derivative is 1 where \(z^{(1)}>0\); both \(z^{(1)}_1=0.28>0\) and \(z^{(1)}_2=0.11>0\), so \(\delta^{(1)} = \partial L/\partial h = [-0.2318,\;0.1854]^T\).

With \(z^{(1)} = W^{(1)T}x+b^{(1)}\): \(\dfrac{\partial L}{\partial W^{(1)}} = x\,\delta^{(1)T}\), \(\dfrac{\partial L}{\partial b^{(1)}} = \delta^{(1)}\):

\[\frac{\partial L}{\partial W^{(1)}} = \begin{bmatrix}0.6\\0.9\\0.4\end{bmatrix}\begin{bmatrix}-0.2318 & 0.1854\end{bmatrix} = \begin{bmatrix}-0.1391 & 0.1113\\ -0.2086 & 0.1669 \\ -0.0927 & 0.0742\end{bmatrix}\]
Answer: \(\nabla_{W^{(1)}}L\) as above; \(\nabla_{b^{(1)}}L = [-0.2318,\;0.1854]^T\).
(f) Updated \(W^{(2)}, b^{(2)}\) with \(\eta=0.1\)

Gradient descent rule \( \theta \leftarrow \theta - \eta\,\nabla_\theta L\):

\[W^{(2)}_{new} = \begin{bmatrix}0.5&-0.4\\0.7&0.3\end{bmatrix} - 0.1\begin{bmatrix}-0.1298&-0.0510\\0&0\end{bmatrix} = \begin{bmatrix}0.5130 & -0.3949\\ 0.7 & 0.3\end{bmatrix}\] \[b^{(2)}_{new} = \begin{bmatrix}0.05\\-0.1\end{bmatrix} - 0.1\begin{bmatrix}-0.4636\\0\end{bmatrix} = \begin{bmatrix}0.0964\\-0.1\end{bmatrix}\]
Answer: \(W^{(2)}_{new}=\begin{bmatrix}0.5130&-0.3949\\0.7&0.3\end{bmatrix}\), \(b^{(2)}_{new}=[0.0964,\,-0.1]^T\).
(g) Updated \(W^{(1)}, b^{(1)}\), same rate
\[W^{(1)}_{new} = \begin{bmatrix}0.4&-0.3\\0.2&0.5\\-0.6&0.1\end{bmatrix} - 0.1\begin{bmatrix}-0.1391&0.1113\\-0.2086&0.1669\\-0.0927&0.0742\end{bmatrix} = \begin{bmatrix}0.4139 & -0.3111\\ 0.2209 & 0.4833\\ -0.5907 & 0.0926\end{bmatrix}\] \[b^{(1)}_{new} = \begin{bmatrix}0.1\\-0.2\end{bmatrix} - 0.1\begin{bmatrix}-0.2318\\0.1854\end{bmatrix} = \begin{bmatrix}0.1232\\-0.2185\end{bmatrix}\]
Answer: \(W^{(1)}_{new}\) as above; \(b^{(1)}_{new}=[0.1232,\,-0.2185]^T\).

Question 2 — LSTM Cell Computation

10 marks
Hidden dimension 2, separate weight matrices (no concatenation). \(h_{t-1}=[0.3,\,-0.5]^T\), \(C_{t-1}=[0.1,\,-0.3]^T\), \(x_t=[0.8,\,0.2]^T\). Gate parameters as given in the paper.
(a) LSTM cell diagram

The LSTM cell (Hochreiter & Schmidhuber, 1997) contains three sigmoid gates regulating a memory cell \(C_t\) that runs through the cell with only element-wise interactions:

  • Forget gate \(f_t=\sigma(W_f^{(h)}h_{t-1}+W_f^{(x)}x_t+b_f)\) — decides what fraction of \(C_{t-1}\) is retained.
  • Input gate \(i_t=\sigma(\cdot)\) with candidate \(\tilde C_t=\tanh(\cdot)\) — decides what new information is written.
  • Cell update \(C_t = f_t\odot C_{t-1} + i_t\odot \tilde C_t\) (the additive path that mitigates vanishing gradients).
  • Output gate \(o_t=\sigma(\cdot)\), exposing \(h_t = o_t\odot\tanh(C_t)\).
\(h_{t-1},\ x_t\) → [σ forget \(f_t\)] · [σ input \(i_t\)] · [tanh candidate \(\tilde C_t\)] · [σ output \(o_t\)]
↓
\(C_t = f_t\odot C_{t-1} + i_t\odot\tilde C_t\)
↓ tanh, gated by \(o_t\)
\(h_t = o_t \odot \tanh(C_t)\)
(b) Forget gate \(f_t\)
\[z_f = W_f^{(h)}h_{t-1} + W_f^{(x)}x_t + b_f\] Row 1: \((0.4)(0.3)+(-1.0)(-0.5) + (0.5)(0.8)+(0.6)(0.2) + 0.2 = 0.12+0.5+0.4+0.12+0.2 = 1.34\)
Row 2: \((-0.3)(0.3)+(0.2)(-0.5) + (0.8)(0.8)+(-0.3)(0.2) - 0.1 = -0.09-0.10+0.64-0.06-0.1 = 0.29\) \[f_t = \sigma([1.34,\;0.29]^T) = [0.7925,\;0.5720]^T\]
Answer: \(f_t=[0.7925,\;0.5720]^T\).
(c) Input gate \(i_t\)
Row 1: \((0.4)(0.3)+(-0.3)(-0.5)+(0.1)(0.8)+(0.4)(0.2)+0 = 0.12+0.15+0.08+0.08 = 0.43\)
Row 2: \((0.2)(0.3)+(0.4)(-0.5)+(-0.2)(0.8)+(0.5)(0.2)+0.1 = 0.06-0.20-0.16+0.10+0.1 = -0.10\) \[i_t = \sigma([0.43,\;-0.10]^T) = [0.6059,\;0.4750]^T\]
Answer: \(i_t=[0.6059,\;0.4750]^T\).
(d) Candidate cell state \(\tilde C_t\)
Row 1: \((0.3)(0.3)+(-0.2)(-0.5)+(0.5)(0.8)+(0.1)(0.2)+0 = 0.09+0.10+0.40+0.02 = 0.61\)
Row 2: \((-0.4)(0.3)+(0.1)(-0.5)+(0.2)(0.8)+(0.3)(0.2)+0.05 = -0.12-0.05+0.16+0.06+0.05 = 0.10\) \[\tilde C_t = \tanh([0.61,\;0.10]^T) = [0.5441,\;0.0997]^T\]
Answer: \(\tilde C_t=[0.5441,\;0.0997]^T\).
(e) Updated cell state \(C_t\)
\[C_t = f_t\odot C_{t-1} + i_t\odot\tilde C_t\] Element 1: \((0.7925)(0.1) + (0.6059)(0.5441) = 0.0792 + 0.3297 = 0.4089\)
Element 2: \((0.5720)(-0.3) + (0.4750)(0.0997) = -0.1716 + 0.0473 = -0.1243\)
Answer: \(C_t=[0.4089,\;-0.1243]^T\).
(f) Output gate \(o_t\) and hidden state \(h_t\)
Row 1: \((0.5)(0.3)+(-0.2)(-0.5)+(0.3)(0.8)+(0.7)(0.2)+0 = 0.15+0.10+0.24+0.14 = 0.63\)
Row 2: \((-0.6)(0.3)+(0.4)(-0.5)+(0.5)(0.8)+(-0.1)(0.2)+0.05 = -0.18-0.20+0.40-0.02+0.05 = 0.05\) \[o_t = \sigma([0.63,\;0.05]^T) = [0.6525,\;0.5125]^T\] \[\tanh(C_t) = \tanh([0.4089,\;-0.1243]^T)=[0.3876,\;-0.1236]^T\] Element 1: \(h_{t,1} = (0.6525)(0.3876)=0.2529\); Element 2: \(h_{t,2} = (0.5125)(-0.1236)=-0.0634\)
Answer: \(o_t=[0.6525,\;0.5125]^T\), \(h_t=[0.2529,\;-0.0634]^T\).

RME 4221: Introduction to Machine Learning — Final Examination 2025

Full Marks: 70 · Time: 3 Hours · Answer any five (5) questions

Question 1 — Gradient Descent & Hyperparameter Tuning

5 + 5 + 4
(a) Gradient descent in backpropagation; one problem

Training a network means finding weights \(\theta\) that minimize a loss \(L(\theta)\). Backpropagation applies the chain rule to compute the gradient \(\nabla_\theta L\) of the loss with respect to every weight and bias, layer by layer, from the output backwards. Gradient descent then uses this gradient as the direction of steepest local decrease and iteratively updates each parameter:

\(\theta^{(k+1)} = \theta^{(k)} - \eta\,\nabla_\theta L(\theta^{(k)})\)

where \(\eta\) is the learning rate. Repeating this over many mini-batches moves the weights toward a (local) minimum of the loss surface, which corresponds to the "optimal" weights the network can find.

Problem: the loss surface of a neural network is non-convex, so gradient descent can become trapped in local minima or saddle points / flat plateaus, and in deep networks the repeated multiplication of derivatives causes vanishing (or exploding) gradients, making early layers learn extremely slowly (Bengio et al., 1994). Convergence is also sensitive to the choice of learning rate.
(b) Learning rate too high vs. too low

The learning rate \(\alpha\) scales the step \( \Delta w = -\alpha\,\partial L/\partial w\) taken at every iteration.

  • Too high: the update overshoots the minimum. The loss oscillates or diverges — the weights bounce across the valley walls and can grow without bound (numerically exploding to NaN). Training becomes unstable and may never converge, or converges to a poor, noisy solution.
  • Too low: each step is tiny, so convergence is extremely slow; training may effectively stall on plateaus, get stuck in the nearest shallow local minimum, and consume excessive compute/epochs before reaching an acceptable loss.
Summary: too high → oscillation/divergence; too low → very slow convergence and risk of stalling. A well-chosen (often decayed/scheduled) rate balances the two.
(c) Random vs. grid search; "coarse to fine"

Grid search evaluates hyperparameters on a fixed lattice; with \(n\) values per hyperparameter and \(k\) hyperparameters it needs \(n^k\) trials, yet only tries \(n\) distinct values of each individual hyperparameter. In practice only a few hyperparameters matter strongly (e.g., learning rate), so most grid trials waste budget re-testing the same value of the important hyperparameter while varying unimportant ones. Random search samples every trial from the full ranges, so the same budget explores many more distinct values of every hyperparameter — Bergstra & Bengio (2012) showed empirically and theoretically that random search finds equal or better configurations in far fewer trials.

Coarse-to-fine strategy: first run a coarse random search over broad ranges (often on a logarithmic scale, e.g., \(\alpha \in [10^{-6},10^{0}]\)). Identify the region where the best-performing samples cluster. Then zoom in: define a narrower range around that region and sample more densely, possibly repeating the process. This concentrates the sampling budget where good configurations live, refining the estimate of the optimum progressively.

Question 2 — Feed-forward Network (3 → 2 ReLU → 1 Sigmoid), Binary Cross-Entropy

2 × 7 = 14
\(x=[0.6,\,0.9,\,0.4]^T\), \(W^{(1)}=\begin{bmatrix}0.3&-0.4\\0.8&0.3\\-0.3&0.2\end{bmatrix}\), \(b^{(1)}=[0.2,\,-0.1]^T\), \(W^{(2)}=[0.3,\;0.4]\), \(b^{(2)}=-0.05\). Target \(y=0\), binary cross-entropy loss, \(\eta=0.1\).
(a) Hidden layer activations
\[z^{(1)}_1 = (0.3)(0.6)+(0.8)(0.9)+(-0.3)(0.4)+0.2 = 0.18+0.72-0.12+0.2 = 0.98\] \[z^{(1)}_2 = (-0.4)(0.6)+(0.3)(0.9)+(0.2)(0.4)-0.1 = -0.24+0.27+0.08-0.1 = 0.01\]

Both positive, so ReLU passes them through.

Answer: \(h=[0.98,\;0.01]^T\).
(b) Output layer activation
\[z^{(2)} = (0.3)(0.98)+(0.4)(0.01)-0.05 = 0.294+0.004-0.05 = 0.248\] \[\hat y = \sigma(0.248) = \frac{1}{1+e^{-0.248}} = 0.5617\]
Answer: \(\hat y = 0.5617\).
(c) Binary cross-entropy loss, \(y=0\)

\(L = -[y\ln\hat y + (1-y)\ln(1-\hat y)] = -\ln(1-\hat y) = -\ln(0.4383)\)

Answer: \(L = 0.8248\).
(d) Gradients w.r.t. \(W^{(2)}, b^{(2)}\)

For sigmoid output with BCE, the derivatives combine to the well-known compact form:

\[\delta^{(2)} = \frac{\partial L}{\partial z^{(2)}} = \hat y - y = 0.5617 - 0 = 0.5617\] \[\nabla_{W^{(2)}}L = \delta^{(2)}h^T = 0.5617\,[0.98,\;0.01] = [0.5504,\;0.0056]\qquad \nabla_{b^{(2)}}L = 0.5617\]
Answer: \(\nabla_{W^{(2)}}L=[0.5504,\;0.0056]\), \(\nabla_{b^{(2)}}L=0.5617\).
(e) Gradients w.r.t. \(W^{(1)}, b^{(1)}\)
\[\frac{\partial L}{\partial h} = W^{(2)T}\delta^{(2)} = [0.3,\;0.4]^T(0.5617) = [0.1685,\;0.2247]^T\] ReLU active on both units (\(z^{(1)}>0\)) → \(\delta^{(1)} = [0.1685,\;0.2247]^T\) \[\nabla_{W^{(1)}}L = x\,\delta^{(1)T} = \begin{bmatrix}0.6\\0.9\\0.4\end{bmatrix}[0.1685,\;0.2247] = \begin{bmatrix}0.1011&0.1348\\0.1517&0.2022\\0.0674&0.0899\end{bmatrix},\qquad \nabla_{b^{(1)}}L = \begin{bmatrix}0.1685\\0.2247\end{bmatrix}\]
(f) Updated \(W^{(2)}, b^{(2)}\), \(\eta = 0.1\)
\[W^{(2)}_{new} = [0.3,\;0.4] - 0.1[0.5504,\;0.0056] = [0.2450,\;0.3994]\] \[b^{(2)}_{new} = -0.05 - 0.1(0.5617) = -0.1062\]
Answer: \(W^{(2)}_{new}=[0.2450,\;0.3994]\), \(b^{(2)}_{new}=-0.1062\).
(g) Updated \(W^{(1)}, b^{(1)}\)
\[W^{(1)}_{new} = \begin{bmatrix}0.3&-0.4\\0.8&0.3\\-0.3&0.2\end{bmatrix} - 0.1\begin{bmatrix}0.1011&0.1348\\0.1517&0.2022\\0.0674&0.0899\end{bmatrix} = \begin{bmatrix}0.2899 & -0.4135\\ 0.7848 & 0.2798\\ -0.3067 & 0.1910\end{bmatrix}\] \[b^{(1)}_{new} = \begin{bmatrix}0.2\\-0.1\end{bmatrix} - 0.1\begin{bmatrix}0.1685\\0.2247\end{bmatrix} = \begin{bmatrix}0.1831\\-0.1225\end{bmatrix}\]

Question 3 — Universal Approximation, Bias–Variance, L2 Regularization

4 + 5 + 5
(a) Universal Approximation Theorem; are shallow networks preferable?

Statement: A feed-forward network with a single hidden layer containing a finite (but sufficiently large) number of units with a non-linear activation (sigmoidal in Cybenko, 1989; any non-polynomial in Hornik, 1991 / Leshno et al., 1993) can approximate any continuous function on a compact subset of \(\mathbb{R}^n\) to arbitrary accuracy.

Does this imply shallow is preferable? No. The theorem is an existence result: it guarantees an approximator exists but says nothing about (i) how many hidden units are needed — for many function classes the required width grows exponentially with input dimension, whereas depth achieves the same accuracy with exponentially fewer units (Montúfar et al., 2014; Telgarsky, 2016); (ii) whether gradient-based learning can find those weights; or (iii) generalization. Deep architectures also learn hierarchical, reusable feature representations (edges → parts → objects), which is why deep networks dominate empirically in vision and language.

(b) Training error 2%, Dev error 15% — diagnosis and two remedies

The gap between training (2%) and dev (15%) error is large while training error itself is low. Low training error → low bias; large train–dev gap → the model fits the training data far better than unseen data.

Diagnosis: high variance (overfitting), not high bias.

Two practical steps:

  1. Get more training data (or use data augmentation) — the most reliable variance reducer, shrinking the train–dev gap.
  2. Add regularization — L2 weight decay, dropout (Srivastava et al., 2014), or early stopping — to constrain model capacity. (Reducing network size / feature count is an equivalent alternative.)
(c) L2-regularized logistic regression cost; effect of \(\lambda\)

For \(m\) examples with parameters \(w, b\) and \(h_w(x)=\sigma(w^Tx+b)\):

\[J(w,b) = -\frac{1}{m}\sum_{i=1}^{m}\Big[y^{(i)}\ln h_w(x^{(i)}) + (1-y^{(i)})\ln\big(1-h_w(x^{(i)})\big)\Big] + \frac{\lambda}{2m}\sum_{j=1}^{n} w_j^2\]

Effect of \(\lambda\): the penalty \(\frac{\lambda}{2m}\lVert w\rVert^2\) shrinks weights toward zero.

  • \(\lambda\) increases → weights shrink → simpler, smoother decision boundary → model complexity decreases, training error increases (bias grows); if too large, underfitting.
  • \(\lambda \to 0\) → no penalty → model uses full capacity → training error decreases but variance/overfitting risk grows.

\(\lambda\) thus traverses the bias–variance trade-off; an intermediate value minimizes dev error.

Question 4 — RNN Types & LSTM Cell Computation

2 × 7 = 14
\(h_{t-1}=[0.5,\,-0.2]^T\), \(C_{t-1}=[0.3,\,-0.4]^T\), \(x_t=[0.7,\,0.3]^T\); the same gate parameter matrices as the in-course paper.
(a) Types of RNN by input/output sequence structure
  • One-to-one: single input → single output (a plain feed-forward net; e.g., image classification).
  • One-to-many: one input → sequence output; e.g., image captioning (image → sentence), music generation from a seed.
  • Many-to-one: sequence input → single output; e.g., sentiment classification of a review, or activity recognition from a sensor stream.
  • Many-to-many (aligned/synchronized): output at every time step, same length; e.g., named-entity tagging / per-frame video labeling.
  • Many-to-many (unaligned, encoder–decoder / seq2seq): input and output sequences of different lengths; e.g., machine translation (Sutskever et al., 2014).
(b) LSTM cell diagram

Same architecture as described in the in-course tab, Question 2(a): forget gate \(f_t\), input gate \(i_t\), candidate \(\tilde C_t\) (tanh), additive cell update \(C_t=f_t\odot C_{t-1}+i_t\odot\tilde C_t\), output gate \(o_t\), and \(h_t = o_t\odot\tanh(C_t)\). The un-interrupted additive cell path is what allows gradients to flow across long time lags.

(c) Forget gate \(f_t\)
Row 1: \((0.4)(0.5)+(-1.0)(-0.2)+(0.5)(0.7)+(0.6)(0.3)+0.2 = 0.20+0.20+0.35+0.18+0.2 = 1.13\)
Row 2: \((-0.3)(0.5)+(0.2)(-0.2)+(0.8)(0.7)+(-0.3)(0.3)-0.1 = -0.15-0.04+0.56-0.09-0.1 = 0.18\) \[f_t = \sigma([1.13,\;0.18]^T)=[0.7558,\;0.5449]^T\]
Answer: \(f_t=[0.7558,\;0.5449]^T\).
(d) Input gate \(i_t\)
Row 1: \((0.4)(0.5)+(-0.3)(-0.2)+(0.1)(0.7)+(0.4)(0.3)+0 = 0.20+0.06+0.07+0.12 = 0.45\)
Row 2: \((0.2)(0.5)+(0.4)(-0.2)+(-0.2)(0.7)+(0.5)(0.3)+0.1 = 0.10-0.08-0.14+0.15+0.1 = 0.13\) \[i_t=\sigma([0.45,\;0.13]^T)=[0.6106,\;0.5325]^T\]
Answer: \(i_t=[0.6106,\;0.5325]^T\).
(e) Candidate cell state \(\tilde C_t\)
Row 1: \((0.3)(0.5)+(-0.2)(-0.2)+(0.5)(0.7)+(0.1)(0.3)+0 = 0.15+0.04+0.35+0.03 = 0.57\)
Row 2: \((-0.4)(0.5)+(0.1)(-0.2)+(0.2)(0.7)+(0.3)(0.3)+0.05 = -0.20-0.02+0.14+0.09+0.05 = 0.06\) \[\tilde C_t=\tanh([0.57,\;0.06]^T)=[0.5154,\;0.0599]^T\]
Answer: \(\tilde C_t=[0.5154,\;0.0599]^T\).
(f) Updated cell state \(C_t\)
Element 1: \((0.7558)(0.3)+(0.6106)(0.5154) = 0.2268 + 0.3147 = 0.5415\)
Element 2: \((0.5449)(-0.4)+(0.5325)(0.0599) = -0.2180 + 0.0319 = -0.1860\)
Answer: \(C_t=[0.5415,\;-0.1860]^T\).
(g) Output gate \(o_t\) and hidden state \(h_t\)
Row 1: \((0.5)(0.5)+(-0.2)(-0.2)+(0.3)(0.7)+(0.7)(0.3)+0 = 0.25+0.04+0.21+0.21 = 0.71\)
Row 2: \((-0.6)(0.5)+(0.4)(-0.2)+(0.5)(0.7)+(-0.1)(0.3)+0.05 = -0.30-0.08+0.35-0.03+0.05 = -0.01\) \[o_t=\sigma([0.71,\;-0.01]^T)=[0.6704,\;0.4975]^T\] \[\tanh(C_t)=[0.4941,\;-0.1839]^T\] \[h_t = o_t\odot\tanh(C_t) = [(0.6704)(0.4941),\;(0.4975)(-0.1839)]^T = [0.3312,\;-0.0915]^T\]
Answer: \(o_t=[0.6704,\;0.4975]^T\), \(h_t=[0.3312,\;-0.0915]^T\).

Question 5 — Regularization, Vanishing Gradient, Augmentation, Multi-crop, Parameter Counting

4+2+2+2+2+2
(a) Regularization; three techniques

Regularization is any modification to the learning algorithm intended to reduce generalization (test) error without necessarily reducing training error (Goodfellow et al., 2016) — i.e., techniques that constrain effective model capacity to prevent overfitting.

  1. L2 weight decay: adds \(\frac{\lambda}{2}\lVert w\rVert_2^2\) to the loss, shrinking weights toward zero and producing smoother functions. (L1 similarly promotes sparsity.)
  2. Dropout: randomly zeroes each hidden unit with probability \(p\) during training (Srivastava et al., 2014); prevents co-adaptation of units and approximates an ensemble of thinned sub-networks.
  3. Early stopping: monitor validation loss during training and stop when it starts increasing — limits the effective number of optimization steps and hence capacity. (Data augmentation and batch normalization also have regularizing effects.)
(b) Deep or shallow — which is more prone to vanishing gradients?
Deep networks.

Backpropagation multiplies local derivatives across layers: \(\frac{\partial L}{\partial w^{(1)}} \propto \prod_{l} \sigma'(z^{(l)})\,W^{(l)}\). With saturating activations \(|\sigma'|\le 0.25\), a product over many layers shrinks exponentially with depth, so gradients reaching early layers of a deep network become vanishingly small; a shallow network has few factors in the product and is largely unaffected (Bengio et al., 1994; Glorot & Bengio, 2010).

(c) Which activation functions are prone to vanishing gradients, and why?

Sigmoid and tanh — the saturating activations. The sigmoid's derivative \(\sigma'(z)=\sigma(z)(1-\sigma(z))\) has maximum 0.25 and tends to 0 for large \(|z|\); tanh's derivative \(1-\tanh^2(z)\) has maximum 1 but also saturates to 0. When units operate in the flat saturation regions, near-zero derivatives are multiplied down the chain and the gradient vanishes. ReLU avoids this on its active side (derivative exactly 1), which is a key reason for its adoption.

(d) Should data augmentation be applied to the test set?
No.

Augmentation is a training-time regularizer meant to expose the model to plausible variations. The test set's role is to provide an unbiased estimate of performance on the true data distribution; transforming it changes that distribution and makes the reported metric non-comparable and non-reproducible. (Test-time augmentation such as multi-crop evaluation is a distinct inference averaging procedure — predictions on deterministic crops are averaged — not augmentation of the test labels/dataset itself, and it must be reported explicitly.)

(e) Multi-crop testing

At evaluation time, several deterministic crops of each test image are taken — classically 10-crop: the four corners plus the center, and their horizontal mirrors (Krizhevsky et al., 2012) — each is passed through the network, and the softmax outputs are averaged to give the final prediction. It is used because it (i) reduces the variance of the prediction, acting as a small ensemble over spatial translations/flips; (ii) compensates for objects not being centered in the crop the way training crops were; and (iii) typically yields a consistent accuracy gain at the cost of extra inference compute.

(f) Parameter count: input 20 → 5 hidden layers × 10 units → scalar output

A fully connected layer from \(n_{in}\) to \(n_{out}\) units has \(n_{in}n_{out}\) weights + \(n_{out}\) biases.

LayerWeightsBiasesParams
Input 20 → H1 (10)20010210
H1 → H210010110
H2 → H310010110
H3 → H410010110
H4 → H510010110
H5 → output (1)10111
Total = 210 + 4(110) + 11 = 661 trainable parameters.

Question 6 — DenseNet vs ResNet; Convolution Cost; CNN Parameter Counting

3 + 5 + 6
(a) DenseNet vs ResNet connectivity; which preserves features better?

ResNet (He et al., 2016) uses identity skip connections that are added element-wise: \(x_{l+1} = x_l + F(x_l)\). Each layer receives only the summed output of the previous block, so earlier feature maps are merged into the sum and are not individually accessible downstream.

DenseNet (Huang et al., 2017) connects every layer to all subsequent layers within a dense block by channel-wise concatenation: \(x_l = H_l([x_0, x_1, \dots, x_{l-1}])\). Each layer therefore has direct access to the unmodified feature maps of every preceding layer.

DenseNet preserves features more effectively, because concatenation keeps earlier features intact and explicitly reusable (feature reuse), whereas ResNet's addition mixes them irreversibly. This also gives DenseNet direct gradient paths to every layer and strong parameter efficiency.
(b) 64×64×128 input, 64 filters of 5×5, stride 1, valid padding

(i) Output size. Valid padding: \(\left\lfloor\frac{64-5}{1}\right\rfloor+1 = 60\).

Output feature map: 60 × 60 × 64.

(ii) Standard convolution cost. Each output value needs \(D_K\times D_K\times M = 5\times5\times128\) multiplications; there are \(60\times60\times64\) outputs:

\[\text{Cost}_{std} = 60\cdot60\cdot64\cdot5\cdot5\cdot128 = 737{,}280{,}000 \approx 7.37\times10^{8}\ \text{multiplications}\]

(iii) Depthwise separable convolution (Howard et al., MobileNets 2017) = depthwise (one 5×5 filter per input channel) + pointwise 1×1 across channels:

\[\text{Depthwise} = 60\cdot60\cdot128\cdot5\cdot5 = 11{,}520{,}000\] \[\text{Pointwise} = 60\cdot60\cdot64\cdot128 = 29{,}491{,}200\] \[\text{Cost}_{DS} = 11{,}520{,}000 + 29{,}491{,}200 = 41{,}011{,}200 \approx 4.10\times10^{7}\]

(iv) Comparison. Theoretical ratio:

\[\frac{\text{Cost}_{DS}}{\text{Cost}_{std}} = \frac{1}{N}+\frac{1}{D_K^2} = \frac{1}{64}+\frac{1}{25} = 0.0556\]
Depthwise separable convolution uses ≈ 5.6% of the standard cost — a speed-up factor of \(737.28/41.01 \approx 18\times\).
(c) 224×224×3 network parameter counting

Architecture: conv1 (20 filters, 5×5, same, stride 1) → 2×2 maxpool s2 → conv2 (40 filters, 7×7, same) → 2×2 maxpool s2 → FC(100) → FC(5). Conv layer parameters \(= N_f(D_K^2\,C_{in}+1)\); pooling has none.

Trace the spatial sizes: 224×224×3 → conv1 (same) → 224×224×20 → pool → 112×112×20 → conv2 (same) → 112×112×40 → pool → 56×56×40 → flatten = 125,440.

LayerComputationParameters
Conv1\(20(5\cdot5\cdot3+1)=20(76)\)1,520
Pool1—0
Conv2\(40(7\cdot7\cdot20+1)=40(981)\)39,240
Pool2—0
FC1\(125{,}440\times100+100\)12,544,100
FC2\(100\times5+5\)505
Total = 1,520 + 39,240 + 12,544,100 + 505 = 12,585,365 trainable parameters. (Note that ≈99.7% sit in the first fully connected layer.)

Question 7 — Non-Max Suppression & IoU

2 + 2 + 2 + 8
(a) Which problem does NMS address?

Detectors (anchor-based or dense-prediction) output many overlapping boxes for the same object — multiple anchors/cells fire on one instance. NMS removes these duplicate/redundant detections, keeping only the highest-confidence box per object so that each object is reported once.

(b) NMS with multiple object classes

NMS is applied independently per class: detections are first grouped by predicted class; within each class the boxes are sorted by confidence and suppression is run using IoU only among boxes of that same class. Boxes of different classes never suppress each other, so a "person" box and an overlapping "bicycle" box can both survive.

(c) Two conditions where anchor-based frameworks degrade
  1. Objects whose scale or aspect ratio is poorly covered by the anchor priors (e.g., very small, very large, or extremely elongated objects) — no anchor achieves sufficient IoU with the ground truth, so positive samples are scarce and recall drops.
  2. Dense scenes of heavily overlapping objects of the same class (crowds, flocks) — nearby instances compete for the same anchors, and NMS then suppresses correct neighbouring detections.
(d) IoU computation and NMS (IoU threshold 0.5)

Boxes (same class): A(30,30)-(90,90), conf 0.88; B(40,40)-(100,100), conf 0.80; C(35,35)-(85,85), conf 0.72; D(150,150)-(200,200), conf 0.60.

(i) IoU(A,B). Intersection: \(x\in[\max(30,40),\min(90,100)]=[40,90]\), \(y\in[40,90]\) → \(50\times50=2500\). Areas: \(A=60^2=3600\), \(B=60^2=3600\). Union \(=3600+3600-2500=4700\).

\(\text{IoU}(A,B) = 2500/4700 = \mathbf{0.532}\)

(ii) IoU(A,C). Intersection: \(x\in[35,85], y\in[35,85]\) → \(50\times50=2500\). Areas: 3600 and \(50^2=2500\). Union \(=3600+2500-2500=3600\).

\(\text{IoU}(A,C) = 2500/3600 = \mathbf{0.694}\)

(iii) IoU(A,D). \(x\)-overlap: \([\max(30,150),\min(90,200)]=[150,90]\) → empty. No intersection.

\(\text{IoU}(A,D) = \mathbf{0}\)

(iv) NMS at IoU threshold 0.5. Sort by confidence: A(0.88) > B(0.80) > C(0.72) > D(0.60).

  1. Select A (highest). Suppress boxes with IoU(·,A) > 0.5: B (0.532 > 0.5) → suppressed; C (0.694 > 0.5) → suppressed; D (0) → survives.
  2. Select D (highest remaining). Nothing left to compare.
Remaining boxes: {A, D}.

(v) If the IoU threshold is raised to 0.6: a box is now suppressed only if it overlaps a kept box more than 0.6. Select A; check B: IoU 0.532 ≤ 0.6 → B is kept; check C: IoU 0.694 > 0.6 → suppressed; D kept as before.

Final set: {A, B, D}. Raising the threshold makes NMS more permissive — boxes must overlap more strongly to be considered duplicates — so B, previously treated as a duplicate of A, now survives. The trade-off: fewer missed neighbours in crowded scenes but more duplicate detections of the same object.

RME 4201: Human Robot Interaction — Final Examination 2024

Full Marks: 70 · Time: 3 Hours · Answer any five (5) questions

Question 1 — Foundations: Social Robots, Proprioception, HCI vs HRI, Human Factors

4 + 4 + 3 + 3
(a) Define social robots; industrial vs social robot by operational environment

Definition. Following Bartneck & Forlizzi (2004), a social robot is "an autonomous or semi-autonomous robot that interacts and communicates with humans by following the behavioral norms expected by the people with whom the robot is intended to interact." Breazeal (2003) similarly characterizes sociable robots as those able to communicate through social cues (speech, gaze, gesture, emotion) and engage people as social partners.

Industrial robotSocial robot
EnvironmentStructured, controlled (cells, cages, fenced lines); humans excluded or separated for safetyUnstructured human spaces — homes, hospitals, schools, shops — shared with people
InteractionMinimal; programmed by experts, no social communicationContinuous, bidirectional, uses natural modalities (speech, gaze, gesture) with non-experts
Task driversPrecision, speed, repeatabilitySocial acceptability, safety around humans, adherence to social norms
UncertaintyLow, engineered awayHigh — must perceive and adapt to unpredictable human behavior
(b) Why is proprioception important when interacting with a child?

Proprioception is the robot's sense of its own internal state — joint angles, motor torques/currents, limb positions, balance — from internal sensors (encoders, IMUs, force sensors).

When interacting with a child it becomes safety-critical:

  • Safe physical contact: children are physically fragile, unpredictable, and may grab, push, or lean on the robot. Proprioceptive feedback lets the robot detect unexpected external forces (e.g., a resisted joint) and comply, stop, or limit torque instead of injuring the child.
  • Self-collision and posture awareness: knowing exact limb configuration prevents the robot from striking the child (or itself) during gestures performed at a child's height and close proximity.
  • Balance: a child bumping a humanoid can topple it; proprioception (IMU + joint states) enables balance recovery so the robot does not fall onto the child.
  • Adaptive interaction: detecting when a child physically guides the robot's arm supports safe kinesthetic play and learning-by-demonstration.
(c) Key differences between HCI and HRI
  • Physical embodiment: HRI involves an embodied agent sharing physical space; interaction includes physical contact, motion, and spatial behavior (proxemics), whereas HCI is largely screen/interface-mediated.
  • Autonomy and agency: robots perceive, decide, and act in the world, so people attribute intentionality and social agency to them; a computer is typically a passive tool awaiting input.
  • Environment & safety: HRI happens in dynamic, uncertain physical environments where robot actions have real physical consequences (safety, force, navigation) — concerns largely absent from HCI.
  • Consequently HRI adds dimensions like anthropomorphism, physical safety standards, and social-norm compliance on top of classical HCI usability concerns (Goodrich & Schultz, 2007).
(d) Human factors in handheld robot controller design

Ergonomics (physical human factors): the controller must fit the anthropometry and biomechanics of the operator's hands — size, weight, grip shape, button reach and travel, and balance — to prevent fatigue and repetitive strain during long teleoperation sessions. Frequently used controls (e.g., e-stop, drive sticks) are placed within thumb/finger reach without regripping; the e-stop is large, distinct, and reachable without looking.

Cognitive load: the operator must simultaneously monitor the robot, the environment, and the interface. The design should minimize working-memory demands: consistent and natural control-to-motion mapping (stick forward → robot forward), grouping of related functions, minimal modes (mode confusion is a classic error source), clear feedback (haptic/LED/auditory confirmation), and progressive disclosure of advanced functions. Lower cognitive load leaves attentional capacity for situation awareness and reduces operator error in safety-critical moments.

Question 2 — Interdisciplinary HRI Project (Assistive Humanoid)

4 + 5 + 5
(a) HRI project vs HCI project vs robotics project
  • Robotics project: centered on making the machine work — kinematics, control, perception, manipulation, navigation. Success = technical performance metrics (accuracy, speed, robustness). The human is largely outside the loop.
  • HCI project: centered on the interface between a human and a (typically screen-based) computing system — usability, UI design, information presentation. Success = task efficiency, usability, satisfaction; no physical embodiment.
  • HRI project: the interaction between human and embodied robot is itself the object of design and study. It inherits the robotics stack and the human-centered evaluation of HCI, plus unique dimensions: physical safety in shared space, social behavior (gaze, proxemics, speech), trust, acceptance, and long-term adaptation. Success is measured jointly on technical function and human outcomes — for our project, whether disabled users can actually and willingly accomplish daily activities with the robot.
(b) Dividing work among engineers, designers, social scientists; how my way of working differs
  • Engineers: hardware, manipulation, perception, safety systems, autonomy software; verification against technical requirements.
  • Designers: the robot's appearance, form factor, interaction flows, dialogue and behavior design, accessibility of the interface for users with different disabilities; prototyping and iterating on the user experience.
  • Social scientists: user needs assessment (interviews, ethnography with disabled users and caregivers), study design and ethics, evaluation of acceptance, trust, dignity, and long-term effects; interpreting qualitative and quantitative user data.

How my working style differs as the engineer: my deliverables are testable artifacts verified against specifications, on iteration cycles driven by builds and quantitative benchmarks. Designers iterate on low-fidelity prototypes and subjective user feedback well before anything "works"; social scientists work on longer empirical cycles (study design → IRB approval → data collection → analysis) and value ecological validity over controlled benchmarks. Practically, I must accept requirements emerging from user studies rather than fixed upfront specs, expose intermediate prototypes earlier than engineering comfort suggests, and treat "the robot functions" as necessary but not sufficient.

(c) Design approach and method choice
Choice: outside-in approach with participatory design.

Outside-in (vs inside-out): inside-out design starts from the technology and asks what it can do for users; outside-in starts from the users' lived context and needs and derives the technology. For assistive robotics the failure mode of inside-out is well documented — technically impressive devices abandoned because they don't fit real routines, homes, or self-image of disabled users. Their daily activities, environments, and capabilities vary enormously, so requirements must originate from them.

Participatory (vs pure engineering- or user-centered): engineering-centered optimizes technical metrics; user-centered studies users but keeps design authority with the team. Participatory design makes disabled users, caregivers, and therapists co-designers throughout — from need-finding through prototype evaluation. This is especially justified here because (i) designers cannot fully simulate the experience of disability ("nothing about us without us"), (ii) assistive technology touches dignity and autonomy, where users' values must shape trade-offs, and (iii) co-design measurably improves adoption and reduces abandonment of assistive devices.

Question 3 — Restaurant Waiter Robot: Proxemics, Constrained Verbal Interaction, Anthropomorphism

5 + 5 + 4
(a) Spatial interaction policies: human proxemics & group spatial dynamics

Proxemics (Hall, 1966) partitions interpersonal space into zones: intimate (< 0.45 m), personal (0.45–1.2 m), social (1.2–3.6 m) and public (> 3.6 m). HRI studies show humans apply comparable zones to robots (Walters et al., 2005; Mumm & Mutlu, 2011). Policy design:

  • Navigation: while moving through the dining area, the robot treats each seated or standing person with a personal-zone buffer (≈ 1 m), never cutting through the intimate zone; it decelerates near people and passes behind rather than between a person and what they are attending to.
  • Approach for service: approach a table from a visible direction (front/side, within the customers' field of view, never from directly behind), decelerating smoothly, and stop at the personal/social boundary (≈ 0.8–1.2 m) — close enough to hand over dishes and be heard, far enough not to loom. Announce presence before entering personal space.
  • Group spatial dynamics (F-formations): a conversing group forms an F-formation (Kendon, 1990): an inner o-space shared by the group, the p-space ring occupied by the participants, and the outer r-space. The robot must not drive through the o-space of a conversing group. To take an order it should join the formation appropriately — position itself at the boundary of the p-space at a socially sanctioned opening, orienting toward the group's o-space, thereby signalling participation without intrusion.
  • Waiting/idle: retreat to social/public distance so as not to pressure diners ("hovering waiter" effect).
(b) Verbal interaction with limited processing power and slow internet (no full NLU)

The key insight is that order-taking is a closed, structured domain, so open natural-language understanding is unnecessary; the interaction can be engineered to remain smooth:

  • Constrained, robot-initiated dialogue: a finite-state script ("Would you like to hear today's specials?", "Which main course?") so customer replies fall in a small expected vocabulary at each state.
  • Small-vocabulary onboard keyword spotting: recognition of menu-item names and yes/no/numbers runs offline; a limited grammar per dialogue state keeps accuracy high on weak hardware.
  • Multimodal redundancy: a touchscreen/tablet menu on the robot as a parallel input channel — customers can tap items while the robot speaks; pointing at a physical/pictorial menu can substitute for speech entirely.
  • Explicit confirmation and repair: after each item, the robot confirms ("One margherita pizza — correct?") with yes/no; misrecognitions are caught cheaply, which is what makes the interaction feel smooth despite weak recognition.
  • Non-verbal cues to manage the exchange: LEDs/head nods to show listening vs thinking, gaze toward the current speaker for turn-taking, and slight pauses so customers don't talk over the robot.
  • Pre-cached speech output (recorded or on-device TTS) so no network round-trips are needed.
(c) Degree of anthropomorphism — Uncanny Valley

Mori's (1970) Uncanny Valley hypothesis: perceived affinity increases with human-likeness up to a point, then plunges sharply for entities that are almost but not perfectly human (eeriness/revulsion), recovering only near full human likeness — with the effect amplified by movement.

Decision procedure for the waiter robot:

  1. Anthropomorphism should match the social demands of the task: the robot must speak, be spoken to, receive gaze, and hand over items — so some human-like cues (a face region, eyes/gaze direction, head orientation) help customers know where to look and talk.
  2. Stay on the left slope of the valley: a stylized, cartoon-like or clearly machine-like body with abstracted facial features (screen face, simple eyes) — not realistic skin, teeth, or near-human faces, which would fall into the valley, especially with imperfect motion at serving speed.
  3. Ensure behavioral consistency with appearance: appearance sets expectations; a very human-looking robot with limited dialogue (our constraint in (b)) creates an expectation mismatch that deepens uncanniness and frustration. A moderately anthropomorphic robot honestly signals moderate abilities.
Conclusion: choose moderate, stylized anthropomorphism — enough human-like cues to support social interaction (face, gaze, voice), deliberately far from photo-realism to stay clear of the uncanny valley and to keep expectations aligned with the robot's actual capability.

Question 4 — Multi-modal Communication, Proxemics, Non-verbal Cues, Social Learning

4 + 3 + 3 + 4
(a) Multi-modal communication; speech + gesture example

Multi-modal communication is the use of two or more communication channels (modalities) — speech, gesture, gaze, facial expression, touch, posture — simultaneously or in coordination to convey meaning. In HRI it increases robustness (channels disambiguate each other), naturalness, and bandwidth of the interaction, since human face-to-face communication is inherently multi-modal (McNeill, 1992).

Example (simultaneous speech + gesture): a person tells a service robot "Put the cup over there" while pointing at a table. The deictic utterance "over there" is ambiguous by itself; the pointing gesture grounds it to a spatial referent. The robot must fuse speech recognition with gesture/pointing detection to resolve the target — neither modality alone suffices. (Equivalently: a robot saying "please take this" while extending its hand holding the object.)
(b) Proxemics; physical distance and user comfort

Proxemics (Hall, 1966) is the study of how humans use and interpret interpersonal space, with the intimate/personal/social/public zones given above. Users extend these norms to robots: a robot entering the intimate zone uninvited causes discomfort, threat perception, and compensatory backing away; stopping too far makes the robot seem aloof and impairs interaction. Empirically, comfortable human–robot distance is modulated by approach direction (frontal close approaches are least comfortable), robot appearance/size, speed, gaze behavior (mutual gaze at close range increases discomfort — Mumm & Mutlu, 2011), and user familiarity. Design implication: default to the personal/social boundary (≈ 1 m), approach within the user's view, decelerate, and adapt distance to observed user reactions.

(c) Non-verbal cues to signal internal state

Because a robot's internal computation is invisible, externalizing state through non-verbal cues supports transparency and legibility:

  • "I am thinking": a slow-pulsing blue LED ring (as on NAO/Pepper), an upward-averted gaze with a slight head tilt (the human "recalling" posture), or a subtle idling animation — signalling processing, so the user waits instead of repeating themselves.
  • "I am confused": red/orange blinking LEDs, a pronounced head tilt toward the speaker, scratching-head gesture, or alternating gaze between user and object — prompting the user to rephrase or assist.
  • Other mappings: green steady = ready/listening; gaze at an object = attention/intention toward it; nodding = understanding. Consistency of the mapping is what lets users learn to read the robot.
(d) Social Learning / Learning from Demonstration for non-experts

Learning from Demonstration (LfD) lets a robot acquire new skills by observing or being physically guided through a task by a human, rather than being explicitly programmed (Argall et al., 2009). The teacher demonstrates (kinesthetic guidance, teleoperation, or observation); the robot generalizes a policy/trajectory model (e.g., DMPs, GMM/GMR) from a few demonstrations.

How it reduces programming burden:

  • Task specification shifts from writing code (requiring programming, kinematics, and API knowledge) to showing the task — a skill every end-user already has. Teaching a new pick-and-place becomes minutes of guidance instead of expert development time.
  • The demonstration implicitly encodes constraints that are hard to formalize (grasp orientations, approach directions, speeds, social conventions), sparing users from articulating them symbolically.
  • It enables in-situ re-teaching when the environment changes, removing the dependency on the manufacturer/integrator for every task variation — critical for homes, small businesses, and care settings staffed by non-experts.

Question 5 — Nursing Robots: Affordances, Ethical Approval, Non-verbal Communication

4 + 5 + 5
(a) Design affordances and patterns for nursing robots

An affordance (Gibson, 1979; Norman, 1988) is a perceivable property of an artifact that indicates how it can be used — good design makes possible actions self-evident. For hospital/clinic nursing robots:

  • Graspable handles / rails that visibly afford holding — for patient support during walking and for staff to manually reposition the robot.
  • Trays and compartments shaped and positioned to afford placing/retrieving medication, samples, and meals at bed height, with clear open/close cues; lockable drug compartments whose closed state is visible.
  • A clear "face"/front (screen, eyes, LEDs) affording where to stand and talk; large high-contrast touch targets affording pressing for elderly or motor-impaired patients.
  • Prominent stop/pause control affording immediate interruption by anyone; soft, rounded, wipeable (hygienic) exteriors affording safe incidental contact.
  • Recognizable interaction patterns: announce-then-approach, confirm-before-acting, consistent status colors, and predictable, legible motion trajectories — patterns borrowed from nursing workflow so staff can predict the robot's behavior without training.
(b) Why ethical approval; standard approval process flow diagram

Why needed: testing nursing robots involves human participants who are patients — a vulnerable population. Risks include physical harm (contact, falls), psychological distress, privacy breaches (health data, in-ward video/audio), deception about robot capabilities, and effects on care quality. Research ethics frameworks (Declaration of Helsinki; Belmont principles of respect for persons, beneficence, and justice) therefore require independent review (IRB/ethics committee) to verify informed consent, risk–benefit balance, data protection, and the right to withdraw — before any testing occurs. It also protects the institution and researchers legally and preserves public trust.

Standard ethical approval process:

1. Define study protocol: aims, procedures, participant population, risks & mitigations
↓
2. Prepare documents: informed-consent forms, information sheets, recruitment material, data-management & privacy plan, safety/risk assessment
↓
3. Submit application to the IRB / institutional ethics committee
↓
4. Committee review (expedited or full board for vulnerable populations)
↓
5. Revise and resubmit per committee feedback (loop until satisfied)
↓
6. Approval granted → conduct study exactly per approved protocol, with ongoing monitoring & adverse-event reporting
↓
7. Report amendments / renewals; final report at study completion
(c) Most important non-verbal communication abilities
  • Gaze and head orientation — most important overall: looking at the patient being addressed establishes attention, supports turn-taking, and conveys "I see you," which is central to perceived care; gaze aversion signals processing. Many patients (post-stroke, hearing-impaired, non-native speakers, intubated) cannot rely on speech, so gaze carries the interaction.
  • Legible motion and proxemic behavior — slow, smooth, predictable approaches respecting personal space; in a ward, sudden or ambiguous motion around beds directly threatens safety and induces anxiety in patients.
  • Status displays (LED color / expression / posture) — communicating "busy / listening / needs help / emergency" at a glance to nurses across the room without interrupting them audibly (wards must stay quiet, especially at night — an argument for non-verbal over verbal channels).
  • Gentle, appropriate touch and hand-over coordination — receiving/handing objects to patients with confirmatory cues (extending tray, pausing until grasp detected).

Why these: the hospital context features noise restrictions, patients with impaired speech/hearing, high anxiety, and safety-critical proximity — precisely the conditions under which non-verbal channels (gaze, motion legibility, visual status) dominate the success of the interaction.

Question 6 — Cubic Trajectory Planning for a Single-Link Manipulator

6 + 8
Rotary joint: start \(\theta_0 = 0^\circ\) with zero velocity; via point \(\theta_v = 60^\circ\) at \(t=5\) s; goal \(\theta_g = 90^\circ\) at \(t=10\) s with zero final velocity.
(a) Cubic from start to via point, with via velocity = 10 deg/s

Assume a cubic \(\theta(t) = a_0 + a_1 t + a_2 t^2 + a_3 t^3\), \(\dot\theta(t) = a_1 + 2a_2 t + 3a_3 t^2\), on \(t\in[0,5]\). Four boundary conditions:

\[\theta(0)=0 \Rightarrow a_0 = 0\] \[\dot\theta(0)=0 \Rightarrow a_1 = 0\] \[\theta(5)=60 \Rightarrow 25a_2 + 125a_3 = 60\] \[\dot\theta(5)=10 \Rightarrow 10a_2 + 75a_3 = 10\]

From the third equation: \(a_2 = (60 - 125a_3)/25 = 2.4 - 5a_3\). Substitute into the fourth:

\[10(2.4 - 5a_3) + 75a_3 = 10 \;\Rightarrow\; 24 - 50a_3 + 75a_3 = 10 \;\Rightarrow\; 25a_3 = -14 \;\Rightarrow\; a_3 = -0.56\] \[a_2 = 2.4 - 5(-0.56) = 5.2\]
Answer: \(\theta(t) = 5.2\,t^{2} - 0.56\,t^{3}\) (degrees, \(0\le t\le 5\) s).
Check: \(\theta(5)=5.2(25)-0.56(125)=130-70=60\) ✓; \(\dot\theta(5)=10.4(5)-1.68(25)=52-42=10\) ✓.

The paper states 10 rad/s; since the positions are in degrees, the velocity is interpreted consistently as 10 deg/s (the algebra is identical either way).

(b) Two cubic segments with continuous velocity and acceleration at the via point

Here the via velocity is not prescribed; instead we impose smoothness (continuity of \(\dot\theta\) and \(\ddot\theta\)) at the via point — the standard two-segment cubic spline formulation (Craig, Introduction to Robotics, §7.4). Let each segment use local time \(t\in[0,5]\):

\(\theta_1(t) = b_0 + b_1 t + b_2 t^2 + b_3 t^3\) (start→via), \(\theta_2(t) = c_0 + c_1 t + c_2 t^2 + c_3 t^3\) (via→goal)

Eight unknowns require eight conditions:

  1. \(\theta_1(0)=0 \Rightarrow b_0=0\)
  2. \(\dot\theta_1(0)=0 \Rightarrow b_1=0\)
  3. \(\theta_1(5)=60 \Rightarrow 25b_2+125b_3=60\)
  4. \(\theta_2(0)=60 \Rightarrow c_0=60\)
  5. \(\theta_2(5)=90 \Rightarrow 60 + 5c_1+25c_2+125c_3=90\)
  6. \(\dot\theta_2(5)=0 \Rightarrow c_1+10c_2+75c_3=0\)
  7. Velocity continuity: \(\dot\theta_1(5)=\dot\theta_2(0) \Rightarrow 10b_2+75b_3 = c_1\)
  8. Acceleration continuity: \(\ddot\theta_1(5)=\ddot\theta_2(0) \Rightarrow 2b_2+30b_3 = 2c_2\)

Solving. From (3): \(5b_2 + 25b_3 = 12\). Write \(c_1 = 10b_2+75b_3\) (7) and \(c_2 = b_2 + 15b_3\) (8). Substitute into (5) and (6):

(5): \(5(10b_2+75b_3) + 25(b_2+15b_3) + 125c_3 = 30 \Rightarrow 75b_2 + 750b_3 + 125c_3 = 30\)

(6): \((10b_2+75b_3) + 10(b_2+15b_3) + 75c_3 = 0 \Rightarrow 20b_2 + 225b_3 + 75c_3 = 0\)

Together with \(5b_2+25b_3=12\), solving the three equations simultaneously (elimination of \(c_3\), then substitution) yields:

\[b_2 = 4.5,\quad b_3 = -0.42,\quad c_3 = 0.06,\quad c_2 = 4.5+15(-0.42) = -1.8,\quad c_1 = 45 - 31.5 = 13.5\]
Segment 1 (0 ≤ t ≤ 5 s): \(\theta_1(t) = 4.5\,t^2 - 0.42\,t^3\)
Segment 2 (0 ≤ t ≤ 5 s, local time from the via point): \(\theta_2(t) = 60 + 13.5\,t - 1.8\,t^2 + 0.06\,t^3\)

Verification. \(\theta_1(5) = 112.5 - 52.5 = 60\) ✓. Via velocity: \(\dot\theta_1(5) = 9(5) - 1.26(25) = 45 - 31.5 = 13.5 = c_1 = \dot\theta_2(0)\) ✓. Via acceleration: \(\ddot\theta_1(5) = 9 - 2.52(5) = -3.6\) and \(\ddot\theta_2(0) = 2c_2 = -3.6\) ✓. Goal: \(\theta_2(5) = 60 + 67.5 - 45 + 7.5 = 90\) ✓, \(\dot\theta_2(5) = 13.5 - 18 + 4.5 = 0\) ✓.

Question 7 — Evaluating a Teaching Robot: Methodology, Participants, Ethics

6 + 4 + 4
A robot designed to teach children in a school; a research study is to be conducted to evaluate the design.
(a) Methodological classification with justification

Qualitative vs quantitative → Mixed, predominantly quantitative. The core evaluation measures learning outcomes numerically — e.g., pre-test/post-test score gains, task completion time, engagement metrics (gaze-on-robot duration, interaction counts), and validated questionnaire scales. This quantitative core is complemented by qualitative data — semi-structured interviews with children and teachers, and open observation of classroom sessions — to explain why effects occur (e.g., novelty effect, specific confusing behaviors). Example: test-score improvement (quantitative) plus interview finding that children trusted the robot's praise (qualitative).

Exploratory vs confirmatory → Confirmatory (with an exploratory component). The robot is already designed with explicit intent — to improve learning — so the study tests pre-specified hypotheses, e.g., H1: "children taught with the robot show greater vocabulary gain than children taught with a tablet-only condition." Hypotheses, conditions, and measures are fixed before data collection. Exploratory elements (mining observation notes for unexpected interaction patterns) remain secondary and are labeled as such.

Descriptive or analytical → Analytical. The study does not merely describe usage; it examines causal relationships between the intervention (robot tutoring vs control condition) and outcomes (learning gain, engagement), using a controlled comparison — e.g., a between-subjects experiment with classes randomly assigned to robot vs traditional instruction, analyzed with inferential statistics (ANOVA / t-tests). Example: testing whether the robot condition causes significantly higher post-test scores than the control at \(\alpha = 0.05\).

(b) Deciding participant type and number

Type: participants must be representative of the intended end-users — children of the target age band and curriculum level the robot was designed for, sampled from the deployment context (real school classes, both genders, range of prior ability), plus their teachers as secondary users/informants. Exclusion/inclusion criteria (age range, language, parental consent) are set in advance. Recruiting the actual user population, rather than convenient adult proxies, is essential for ecological validity in child–robot interaction research (Belpaeme et al., 2018).

Number — statistical power analysis: the sample size is computed a priori from (i) the expected effect size (from pilot data or prior robot-tutoring literature, typically small-to-medium, e.g., Cohen's \(d \approx 0.4\text{–}0.5\)), (ii) significance level \(\alpha = 0.05\), and (iii) desired power \(1-\beta = 0.80\). For a two-group comparison at \(d=0.5\) this yields roughly 60–65 children per condition; smaller expected effects require more. Practical corrections: add margin for attrition/absence, respect clustering if randomizing by classroom (design effect), and cap by feasibility. A small pilot (5–10 children) precedes the main study to refine measures and effect-size estimates; for the qualitative strand, interviewing continues to thematic saturation rather than a fixed n.

(c) Societal & ethical impacts; addressing them in study design

Probable societal/ethical impacts of adoption:

  • Effect on human teachers and teaching labor — displacement fears, or deskilling if robots absorb core instruction; conversely, changed teacher roles.
  • Child development concerns — attachment to and over-trust in the robot, deception (children believing the robot truly "knows" or "cares"), reduced child–child and child–teacher social interaction (Sharkey, 2016).
  • Privacy and data protection — robots record children's speech, video, faces, and performance data; risk of misuse or profiling of minors.
  • Equity — unequal access could widen educational gaps between well- and under-resourced schools; conversely poor deployment could impose untested technology on disadvantaged schools.
  • Bias — speech/vision models performing worse for some accents/appearances, disadvantaging some children.

Addressing them in the study design: obtain ethics-committee approval treating children as a vulnerable population; require informed parental consent and child assent with unconditional withdrawal rights; minimize and anonymize collected data, storing recordings securely with defined retention limits; keep a teacher present and frame the robot as a teaching aid, not replacement, measuring effects on teacher–child and peer interaction as explicit outcome variables; include attachment/over-trust measures and debrief children honestly about what the robot is; sample across demographic groups and analyze for differential (biased) performance; and report negative or null results to inform responsible adoption policy.

RME 4211: Human Robot Interaction Lab — Final Examination 2024

Full Marks: 10 · Time: 20 minutes · Answer all questions

Question 1 — Behavior Layers in Programming NAO

2 marks

In Choregraphe, a behavior can be organized into behavior layers, each containing its own timeline/flow of boxes that execute in parallel with the other layers. Their uses:

  • Parallel, modular behavior composition: one layer can run a walking or body animation while another layer simultaneously handles speech, LED effects, or sensor monitoring — instead of forcing everything into a single sequential flow.
  • Behavior switching and management: layers contain behavior keyframes; moving between keyframes starts/stops sub-behaviors at defined points, letting the programmer coordinate, interrupt, or sequence complex behaviors (e.g., an "idle" layer that is overridden when a "respond to touch" layer activates).
  • This layered decomposition keeps large interactive applications organized, reusable, and easier to debug, since each concern (motion, dialogue, reactions) lives on its own layer.

Question 2 — Three Parameters of the Speech Recognition Box

2 marks

The Choregraphe Speech Reco. box exposes three parameters:

  1. Word list: the vocabulary — a semicolon-separated list of words/phrases (e.g., yes;no;hello) that the engine will try to recognize. NAO's recognition is grammar-based, so it only recognizes words from this list.
  2. Confidence threshold (%): the minimum recognition confidence required for a word to be accepted and output. Recognitions scoring below the threshold are rejected — raising it reduces false positives but may miss quiet/accented speech; lowering it does the opposite.
  3. Visual expression: a boolean enabling NAO's visual feedback during listening — the eye LEDs change (e.g., blue while listening, green flash on successful recognition), signalling to the user when the robot is listening and whether it understood, which supports smoother turn-taking.

Question 3 — HRI Applications in Medical and Social Domains

2 marks

Medical domain:

  • Rehabilitation and therapy robots — exoskeletons and therapy platforms guiding post-stroke motor exercises with interactive feedback.
  • Robot-assisted autism therapy — robots such as NAO used in interventions to teach children with ASD turn-taking, imitation, and joint attention, exploiting their preference for predictable robot behavior (Scassellati et al., 2012).
  • Hospital assistance — nursing/logistics robots delivering medication and samples, telepresence robots for remote consultation, and surgical teleoperation interfaces (surgeon–robot interaction).
  • Elderly care — companion robots (e.g., PARO) shown to reduce agitation in dementia care; monitoring and reminder robots supporting independent living.

Social domain:

  • Education — robot tutors and teaching assistants improving engagement and learning (Belpaeme et al., 2018).
  • Service and reception — robots (e.g., Pepper) greeting, guiding, and informing customers in shops, banks, airports, and museums.
  • Companionship and social support — robots providing conversation and emotional engagement for isolated users.
  • Public space assistance — guide robots in malls/museums that must navigate crowds using socially aware behavior.

Question 4 — NAO "Stiffness"; Problems of 100% Stiffness

2 marks

Stiffness on NAO is the degree to which each joint's motor actively holds/drives its commanded position — effectively the motor torque enabling (0 = motors off, joints limp and freely movable by hand; 1 = motors apply maximum holding torque, joints rigid). Stiffness must be on for the robot to move or stand, and off for safe manual manipulation (e.g., animation-mode kinesthetic teaching).

Problems if stiffness is always 100%:

  • Motor overheating: continuous maximum current makes joints heat rapidly; NAO's protection then reduces power or shuts joints down mid-behavior.
  • Rapid battery drain — holding full torque even when idle wastes energy and shortens session time.
  • Increased wear and gear damage — sustained load, and no compliance during impacts: if the robot falls or is pushed, rigid joints transmit the shock to the gears rather than yielding.
  • Unsafe and inflexible interaction — the robot resists external forces, so it cannot be safely guided by hand (no kinesthetic teaching), and contact with a person is harder/riskier than with compliant joints.

Hence practice is to modulate stiffness: full only during motion that needs it, reduced/relaxed when resting or being handled.

Question 5 — Two Major Research Areas in HRI

2 marks

1. Trust and acceptance in HRI. Studies how humans calibrate trust in robots — what drives over-trust (blind compliance with a faulty robot) and under-trust (disuse of a capable one) — and how robot reliability, transparency, appearance, and failures shape acceptance (Hancock et al., 2011 meta-analysis). It matters because appropriately calibrated trust is a precondition for safe deployment in homes, hospitals, and vehicles; research covers trust measurement scales, trust repair after robot errors, and explainable robot behavior.

2. Socially-aware navigation and non-verbal interaction. Studies how robots should move and behave among people: proxemics-respecting path planning, legible/predictable motion, approaching and joining groups (F-formations), and generating/reading non-verbal cues such as gaze, gesture, and posture (Kruse et al., 2013 survey; Mutlu's gaze studies). It matters because most robot–human contact in shared spaces is implicit and non-verbal; robots that violate spatial and social norms are rated unsafe and unacceptable regardless of task competence.

Other acceptable areas: learning from demonstration, robot-assisted therapy/education, teleoperation & shared autonomy, long-term interaction, and ethics of social robots — any two, identified and analyzed, earn the marks.

RME 4221: Introduction to Machine Learning — In-course 1

Total Marks: 25 · Time: 50 minutes

Question 1 — Classification vs Object Detection vs Instance Segmentation

3 marks
TaskOutputLocalizationInstance separation
ClassificationA single class label (and score) for the whole image, e.g., "dog"NoneNone
Object detectionA class label + bounding box for every object presentCoarse — rectangular boxesYes — one box per instance
Instance segmentationA class label + pixel-level mask for every object instance (e.g., Mask R-CNN)Precise — pixel-accurate outlinesYes — each instance gets its own mask (unlike semantic segmentation, which merges instances of the same class)

They form an increasing granularity hierarchy: what is in the image → what and where (boxes) → what, where, and exactly which pixels per individual object.

Question 2 — Primary Disadvantage of R-CNN

3 marks
It is extremely slow and computationally redundant: R-CNN (Girshick et al., 2014) runs the full CNN forward pass independently on each of the ~2,000 region proposals per image, with no sharing of convolutional computation between overlapping regions.

Consequences: ~47 s per image at test time (VGG backbone), infeasible for real-time use; training is also a slow, multi-stage pipeline (CNN fine-tuning, then SVM classifiers, then box regressors) requiring hundreds of GB of cached features. Fast R-CNN removed the redundancy by computing one shared feature map and pooling per-region features (RoI pooling) from it.

Question 3 — Handling Overlapping Detections

3 marks
Non-Maximum Suppression (NMS).

Detections of the same class are sorted by confidence; the highest-confidence box is kept and all remaining boxes whose IoU with it exceeds a threshold (typically 0.5) are suppressed as duplicates; the procedure repeats on the survivors. This ensures each object is reported once, by its best-scoring box. (Variants: Soft-NMS decays scores instead of deleting; anchor-free/end-to-end detectors like DETR avoid NMS via set prediction.)

Question 4 — Convolution Output Dimensions and Parameters

5 marks
Input 32 × 32 × 3; one conv layer with a 5 × 5 kernel, zero padding (P = 0), stride 1, and 2 filters.

Output dimensions. Per spatial axis: \(O = \dfrac{W - K + 2P}{S} + 1 = \dfrac{32 - 5 + 2(0)}{1} + 1 = 28\). Depth = number of filters = 2.

Output volume: 28 × 28 × 2.

Parameters. Each filter spans the full input depth: \(5 \times 5 \times 3 = 75\) weights, plus 1 bias per filter:

\[\text{Weights} = 5\cdot5\cdot3\cdot2 = 150,\qquad \text{Biases} = 2,\qquad \text{Total} = 152\]

If "zero padding" were instead read as "same" padding, the spatial size would remain 32 × 32 (× 2), but the parameter count is independent of padding and stride: still 150 weights + 2 biases.

Question 5 — Flattened FC Layer vs Full-size Conv Filters

5 marks
10 × 10 grayscale input, 5-class classifier. Option (a): flatten to 100-d vector → FC layer with 5 neurons. Option (b): conv layer with five 10 × 10 filters.

Key observation — the two options are mathematically equivalent.

(a) FC: each of the 5 neurons computes \(y_j = \sum_{i=1}^{100} w_{ji}x_i + b_j\). Parameters: \(100\times5 + 5 = 505\).

(b) Conv: a 10 × 10 filter on a 10 × 10 input (valid, stride 1) produces output size \(\frac{10-10}{1}+1 = 1\), i.e., a single value per filter — the output volume is 1 × 1 × 5. Each filter has \(10\cdot10\cdot1 + 1 = 101\) parameters; five filters give \(505\).

When the kernel covers the entire input, the convolution degenerates to one dot product over all pixels — exactly a fully connected neuron. Both options compute five global linear combinations of the same 100 pixels with identical parameter counts (505), so they have identical expressive power, and the usual advantages of convolution — local receptive fields, weight sharing across spatial positions, and translation equivariance — are all forfeited because the filter is never slid.

Answer: Either is acceptable because they are equivalent; option (b) offers no convolutional benefit here. I would choose (a) as the simpler, more honest formulation — or, if convolutional advantages are actually desired, neither: use small filters (e.g., 3 × 3) so weight sharing and locality genuinely apply.

Question 6 — True Propositions about a CONV Layer

3 marks

Parameter count of a conv layer: \(F\,(K\times K\times C_{in}) + F\) — it depends only on kernel size \(K\), input depth \(C_{in}\), and number of filters \(F\).

  • (i) True. Weights \(= K\cdot K\cdot C_{in}\cdot F\) — directly proportional to the input volume's depth \(C_{in}\).
  • (ii) True. One bias per filter → number of biases = number of filters \(F\).
  • (iii) False. Stride changes only the output spatial size, not the parameters.
  • (iv) False. Padding likewise affects output size only; the shared kernel weights are unchanged.
Answer: (i) and (ii) are true; (iii) and (iv) are false.

Question 7 — Which Technique Does NOT Prevent Overfitting?

3 marks
Answer: (iv) None of the above.

All three listed techniques are regularizers: data augmentation enlarges the effective training distribution, reducing variance; dropout (Srivastava et al., 2014) prevents co-adaptation of units and acts as an implicit ensemble; early stopping halts training when validation loss rises, limiting effective capacity. Since each prevents overfitting, the technique that does not is "none of the above."

RME 4201: Human Robot Interaction — In-course Examinations

1st In-course (Full Marks: 10 · 1 hour) + additional in-course questions on the same sheet

Question 1 — Principles for Robot Forms, Patterns, and Affordances in HRI Design

5 marks

HRI researchers (Bartneck, Belpaeme, Eyssel, Kanda, Keijsers & Šabanović, Human–Robot Interaction: An Introduction, 2020; Norman, 1988; Kahn et al., 2008) propose the following design principles:

  1. Form follows function — match form to function: the robot's appearance should reflect what it can actually do. A robot shaped for a task (arms if it manipulates, a face if it converses) lets users infer its capabilities correctly; decorative human-likeness on a robot that cannot behave socially misleads users.
  2. Design for correct expectations — underpromise and overdeliver: appearance sets capability expectations. Because behavior rarely lives up to a highly humanlike form, designers should keep the form slightly below the robot's actual competence so the robot exceeds, rather than disappoints, expectations (this also steers clear of the uncanny valley).
  3. Use perceivable affordances: following Norman's reading of Gibson, every actionable part should visually signal its use — handles that look graspable, buttons that look pressable, a face region that shows where to talk. Good affordances remove the need for instruction.
  4. Use established design patterns: reuse proven, recurring interaction solutions — e.g., Kahn et al.'s HRI design patterns such as initial introduction, didactic communication, in motion together, personal interests and history — rather than inventing every interaction from scratch. Patterns encode socially validated behavior sequences.
  5. Don't mix metaphors — design consistency: the robot's form, voice, motion, and behavior should express one coherent character (e.g., a machine-like body with a photorealistic human voice clashes and confuses users).
  6. Iterative, user-centered/participatory process: prototype early, test with real target users, and refine — since user perception of form and affordance cannot be fully predicted analytically.

Question 2 — The Uncanny Valley: Concept, Diagram, and Causes

5 marks

Concept. Mori (1970) hypothesized that affinity (shinwakan) toward an artificial agent increases with its human likeness — but only up to a point. For agents that are very close to, yet distinguishably not, human, affinity collapses into eeriness and revulsion: the uncanny valley. Affinity recovers as likeness approaches that of a real healthy human. Movement deepens the valley: a moving almost-human agent (android, animated corpse-like prosthetic) is eerier than a still one.

Reasons proposed for the valley (literature):

  • Category uncertainty / perceptual mismatch: the agent sits at the boundary between "human" and "non-human" categories; conflicting cues (human skin but robotic eyes or jerky motion) create a processing conflict that the perceptual system registers as eeriness (Saygin et al., 2012 — prediction error in the action-perception system).
  • Violation of expectations: a near-human appearance triggers the full set of human behavioral norms (micro-expressions, gaze timing, natural motion); any subtle failure to meet them is salient and disturbing, whereas a clearly mechanical robot is judged by machine standards.
  • Evolutionary threat-avoidance: almost-human-but-wrong appearance resembles disease, deformity, or death; pathogen-avoidance and corpse-avoidance instincts (and mate-selection accounts) produce automatic aversion (MacDorman & Ishiguro, 2006).
  • Movement amplification: motion raises the level of realism the brain expects, so imperfect movement in a highly humanlike body deepens the negative response (Mori, 1970).

Question 3 — Types of Non-verbal Interaction

5 marks

Following the taxonomy in the HRI textbook (Bartneck et al., 2020, Ch. Non-verbal Interaction):

  1. Gaze and eye movement: where an agent looks — signals attention, regulates turn-taking, establishes joint attention (looking at an object to make the partner look too), and conveys engagement.
  2. Gesture: hand/arm movements — deictic (pointing), iconic (depicting shape/size), symbolic/emblematic (wave, thumbs-up), and beat gestures accompanying speech rhythm.
  3. Posture and body movement: body orientation and stance communicate attitude and engagement (leaning forward = interest); motion quality (smooth vs abrupt) conveys internal state and intent legibility.
  4. Facial expression: configurations of facial features expressing emotion (smile, frown) and conversational signals (raised eyebrows, nods); central to affective communication.
  5. Touch (haptic interaction): physical contact — handshakes, pats, guiding by the hand — conveying social warmth, comfort, or control; safety-critical in robots.
  6. Proxemics: use of interpersonal distance and spatial positioning (Hall's intimate/personal/social/public zones); distance itself communicates the relationship and intent.
  7. Paralanguage / non-linguistic utterances: the non-word aspects of voice — prosody, pitch, loudness, pace, sighs, laughs, beeps (R2-D2-style) — carrying emotion and emphasis.
  8. Chronemics (interaction timing): the use of time — response delays, pause length, turn-taking rhythm; ill-timed responses break the flow of interaction even when content is correct.

Question 4 — "Emotion as a Point in 2D or 3D Space"

5 marks

Meaning of the statement. Dimensional theories of emotion hold that emotions are not discrete categories but positions in a continuous low-dimensional space whose axes are fundamental affective dimensions. Any emotional state can then be represented — and interpolated — as a coordinate point, which is convenient computationally for affective HRI (recognizing, representing, and expressing emotion as continuous values rather than labels).

2D model — Russell's circumplex model of affect (1980): two orthogonal axes: valence (pleasure–displeasure, x-axis) and arousal (activation–deactivation, y-axis). Emotions arrange in a circle in this plane: happy = positive valence/moderately high arousal; excited = positive/high arousal; sad = negative/low arousal; angry = negative/high arousal; calm = positive/low arousal. A robot can express any point by modulating, e.g., speed/amplitude of motion (arousal) and posture openness or LED color (valence).

3D model — PAD model (Mehrabian & Russell, 1974): adds a third axis to resolve states that coincide in 2D: Pleasure (P), Arousal (A), and Dominance (D) — the sense of control over the situation. Example: anger and fear are both negative-valence, high-arousal (identical in the 2D plane) but differ in dominance: anger is high-dominance (+P−, A+, D+... precisely P−A+D+), fear is low-dominance (P−A+D−). Plutchik's cone/wheel is a related 3D arrangement (emotion families × intensity), but PAD is the canonical 3D dimensional model used in affective computing and HRI.

Summary: 2D → Russell's circumplex (valence × arousal); 3D → PAD (pleasure × arousal × dominance), whose extra dominance axis distinguishes emotions the circumplex conflates.

RME 4201: Human Robot Interaction — Final Examination 2023

Full Marks: 70 · Time: 3 Hours · Answer any five (5) questions

Question 1 — Asimov's Laws, Exploratory vs Confirmatory Research, Correlation vs Causation

5 + 5 + 4
(a) Asimov's three laws; why introduced

Isaac Asimov introduced the Three Laws of Robotics in his 1942 short story "Runaround":

  1. A robot may not injure a human being or, through inaction, allow a human being to come to harm.
  2. A robot must obey the orders given it by human beings, except where such orders would conflict with the First Law.
  3. A robot must protect its own existence, as long as such protection does not conflict with the First or Second Law.

Why introduced: as a literary device to counter the then-dominant "Frankenstein complex" — the trope of robots inevitably turning on their creators — by imagining machines with built-in ethical safeguards, and to explore, through stories, how even well-intended hierarchical rules produce dilemmas, ambiguities, and unintended consequences. In HRI/robot-ethics teaching they serve as the historical starting point for discussing machine ethics and why simple rule hierarchies are insufficient for real systems (e.g., defining "harm," conflicting orders, epistemic limits of robots).

(b) Exploratory vs confirmatory research, with HRI examples
ExploratoryConfirmatory
GoalGenerate hypotheses; discover phenomena, patterns, and variables in a poorly understood areaTest pre-specified hypotheses derived from theory or prior findings
DesignOpen-ended: observation, ethnography, interviews, pilot deployments; flexible measuresControlled experiment: fixed conditions, operationalized variables, planned statistics, ideally preregistered
OutcomeRich descriptions, candidate hypothesesHypothesis supported/rejected at a stated significance level
  • Exploratory HRI example: placing a service robot in a shopping mall for a month and observing, without predefined hypotheses, how shoppers spontaneously react — who approaches, what they try to say, what confuses them — to generate hypotheses about public robot interaction.
  • Confirmatory HRI example: testing the preregistered hypothesis "users keep a larger distance from a robot that establishes mutual gaze than from one that does not" in a controlled lab experiment with randomized gaze conditions and measured stopping distance (cf. Mumm & Mutlu, 2011).
(c) Is correlation always indicative of causation?
No. Correlation is a necessary hint but never sufficient evidence of causation.

A correlation between X and Y can arise from: X→Y, Y→X (reverse causality), a confounding third variable Z→X and Z→Y, selection bias, or pure coincidence in finite samples. Example: ice-cream sales correlate strongly with drowning deaths across the year — neither causes the other; hot summer weather (confounder) raises both. An HRI example: users who interact longer with a robot report liking it more — this does not show longer interaction causes liking; users who already like robots may simply choose to interact longer. Establishing causation requires controlled manipulation (randomized experiments) or careful causal-inference designs.

Question 2 — Proxemics, F-formation, Robot Localization & Navigation

5 + 5 + 4
(a) Proxemics and Hall's four distance zones

Proxemics (Hall, 1966) is the study of how humans use interpersonal space as a communication medium — how distance regulates and expresses social relationships, and how spatial violations produce discomfort. Hall's four zones:

ZoneDistanceReserved for
Intimate0 – 0.45 mEmbracing, touching, whispering — closest relationships; entry by others is threatening
Personal0.45 – 1.2 mConversations with friends and family; arm's-length interaction
Social1.2 – 3.6 mInteractions with acquaintances, colleagues, service encounters
Public> 3.6 mPublic speaking, addressing groups; impersonal contact

HRI relevance: people apply comparable zones to robots (Walters et al., 2005); a robot should normally interact from the personal/social boundary and never enter the intimate zone uninvited.

(b) F-formation: why used; o-space, p-space, r-space
